<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome and transcriptome analysis of snake gourd provide insights into its evolution and fruit development and ripening"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-12-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Snake gourd (Trichosanthes anguina L.), which belongs to the Cucurbitaceae family, is a popular ornamental and food crop species with medicinal value and is grown in many parts of the world. Although progress has been made in its genetic improvement, the organization, composition, and evolution of the snake gourd genome remain largely unknown. Here, we report a high-quality genome assembly for snake gourd, comprising 202 contigs, with a total size of 919.8&#8201;Mb and an N50 size of 20.1&#8201;Mb. These findings indicate that snake gourd has one of the largest genomes of Cucurbitaceae species sequenced to date. The snake gourd genome assembly harbors 22,874 protein-coding genes and 80.0% of the genome consists of repetitive sequences. Phylogenetic analysis reveals that snake gourd is closely related to sponge gourd but diverged from their common ancestor ~33&#8211;47 million years ago. The genome sequence reported here serves as a valuable resource for snake gourd genetic research and comparative genomic studies in Cucurbitaceae and other plant species. In addition, fruit transcriptome analysis reveals the candidate genes related to quality traits during snake gourd fruit development and provides a basis for future research on snake gourd fruit development and ripening at the transcript level."/>

Although progress has been made in its genetic improvement, the organization, composition, and evolution of the snake gourd genome remain largely unknown. The genome sequence reported here serves as a valuable resource for snake gourd genetic research and comparative genomic studies in Cucurbitaceae and other plant species. In addition, fruit transcriptome analysis reveals the candidate genes related to quality traits during snake gourd fruit development and provides a basis for future research on snake gourd fruit development and ripening at the transcript level."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Snake gourd (<i>Trichosanthes anguina</i> L.), which belongs to the Cucurbitaceae family, is a popular ornamental and food crop species with medicinal value and is grown in many parts of the world. Although progress has been made in its genetic improvement, the organization, composition, and evolution of the snake gourd genome remain largely unknown. Here, we report a high-quality genome assembly for snake gourd, comprising 202 contigs, with a total size of 919.8âMb and an N50 size of 20.1âMb. These findings indicate that snake gourd has one of the largest genomes of Cucurbitaceae species sequenced to date. The snake gourd genome assembly harbors 22,874 protein-coding genes and 80.0% of the genome consists of repetitive sequences. Phylogenetic analysis reveals that snake gourd is closely related to sponge gourd but diverged from their common ancestor ~33â47 million years ago. The genome sequence reported here serves as a valuable resource for snake gourd genetic research and comparative genomic studies in Cucurbitaceae and other plant species. In addition, fruit transcriptome analysis reveals the candidate genes related to quality traits during snake gourd fruit development and provides a basis for future research on snake gourd fruit development and ripening at the transcript level.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>As the second largest vegetable family, the Cucurbitaceae family comprises one of the most genetically diverse groups of plants. The members of this family are prevalent in tropical regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Schaefer, H. &amp; Renner, S. S. Phylogenetic relationships in the order Cucurbitales and a new classification of the gourd family (Cucurbitaceae). Taxon 60, 122â138 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d1853e658">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Devi, N. Medicinal values of Trichosanthus cucumerina L. (snake gourd) - a review. Br. J. Pharm. Res. 16, 1â10 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1853e661">2</a></sup> and many species are now cultivated worldwide as food crops. Snake gourd (<i>Trichosanthes anguina</i> L.; 2<i>n</i>â=â2Ãâ=â22) is a diploid annual woody climber (liana) of the genus <i>Trichosanthes</i> in the family Cucurbitaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Devi, N. Medicinal values of Trichosanthus cucumerina L. (snake gourd) - a review. Br. J. Pharm. Res. 16, 1â10 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1853e674">2</a></sup>. This species is commonly called viper gourd, snake tomato, or long tomato, and has several synonyms (e.g., <i>Trichosanthes cucumerina</i> L.). Snake gourd originated in India or the Indo-Malayan region in tropical Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adebooye, O. C. Phytoconstituents and anti-oxidant activity of the pulp of snake tomato (Tricosanthes cucumerina). Afr. J. Tradit. Complement. Altern. Med. 5, 173â179 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1853e682">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Roy, R. P., Saran, S. &amp; Dutt, B. Cytogenetics of the cucurbitaceae. Dev. Plant Genet. Breed. 2, 181â199 (1991)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d1853e685">4</a></sup> and is widely distributed in Asian countries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Arawwawala, M., Thabrew, I. &amp; Arambewela, L. Antidiabetic activity of Trichosanthes cucumerina in normal and streptozotocinâinduced diabetic rats. Int. J. Biol. Sci. 3, 287â296 (2009)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d1853e689">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liyanage, R., Nadeeshani, H., Jayathilake, C., Visvanathan, R. &amp; Wimalasiri, S. Comparative analysis of nutritional and bioactive properties of aerial parts of snake gourd (Trichosanthes cucumerina Linn.). Int. J. Food Sci. 2016, 1â7 (2016)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1853e689_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ojiako, O. A. &amp; Igwe, C. U. The nutritive, anti-nutritive and hepatotoxic properties of Trichosanthes anguina (snake tomato) fruits from Nigeria. Pak. J. Nutr. 7, 85â89 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d1853e692">7</a></sup>. Its green, tender stems, leaves, and fruits are consumed as edible vegetables<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Devi, N. Medicinal values of Trichosanthus cucumerina L. (snake gourd) - a review. Br. J. Pharm. Res. 16, 1â10 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1853e696">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Liyanage, R., Nadeeshani, H., Jayathilake, C., Visvanathan, R. &amp; Wimalasiri, S. Comparative analysis of nutritional and bioactive properties of aerial parts of snake gourd (Trichosanthes cucumerina Linn.). Int. J. Food Sci. 2016, 1â7 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1853e699">6</a></sup>, which have high nutritional value, because they are rich in vitamins, essential minerals, dietary fiber, and other nutrients, and are a wholesome, healthy addition to diets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ojiako, O. A. &amp; Igwe, C. U. The nutritive, anti-nutritive and hepatotoxic properties of Trichosanthes anguina (snake tomato) fruits from Nigeria. Pak. J. Nutr. 7, 85â89 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d1853e703">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Rana, N. P. &amp; Pandit, M. K. Studies on genetic variability, character association and path analysis in snake gourd (Trichosanthes anguina L.) genotypes. J. Crop Weed 7, 91â96 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d1853e706">8</a></sup>. The fruits of snake gourd are frequently consumed when immature. As the fruits mature, the rind and flesh turn red, and the red flesh is used as a tomato substitute<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Devi, N. Medicinal values of Trichosanthus cucumerina L. (snake gourd) - a review. Br. J. Pharm. Res. 16, 1â10 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1853e710">2</a></sup>. Snake gourd fruits can grow to 1.5âm in length, are serpentine in shape (hence the name snake gourd), and are used for ornamental purposes when they are mature. Their functional constituents include flavonoids, Î²-carotenoids, lycopene, and phenolic acids, which have beneficial pharmacological and therapeutic effects for humans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Devi, N. Medicinal values of Trichosanthus cucumerina L. (snake gourd) - a review. Br. J. Pharm. Res. 16, 1â10 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1853e715">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sandhya, S., Vinod, K. R., Sekhar, J. C., Aradhana, R. &amp; Nath, V. S. An updated review on Tricosanthes cucumerina L. Int. J. Pharm. Sci. Rev. Res. 1, 56â60 (2010)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d1853e718">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yusuf, A. A., Folarin, O. M. &amp; Bamiro, F. O. Chemical composition and functional properties of snake gourd (Tricosanthes cucumerina) seed flour. Niger. Food J. 25, 36â45 (2007)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d1853e718_1">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sathesh, K. S., Ravi, K. B. &amp; Krishna, M. G. Hepatoprotective effect of Trichosanthes cucumerina Var cucumerina L. on carbon tetrachloride induced liver damage in rats. J. Ethnopharmacol. 123, 347â350 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d1853e721">11</a></sup>. For these reasons, snake gourd is a potentially economically important crop species with food, medicinal and ornamental value, and is worthy of further study and scientific research.</p><p>In the past decade, owing to the rapid advances in sequencing technology and bioinformatic algorithms, reference genomes of a number of cucurbit species have been assembled, including cucumber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275â1281 (2009)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d1853e728">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, Z. et al. RNA-Seq improves annotation of protein-coding genes in the cucumber genome. BMC Genomics 12, 540 (2011)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d1853e728_1">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Woycicki, R. et al. The genome sequence of the North-European cucumber (Cucumis sativus L.) unravels evolutionary adaptation mechanisms in plants. PLoS ONE 6, e22728 (2011)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1853e728_2">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang, L. et al. Chromosome rearrangements during domestication of cucumber as revealed by high-density genetic mapping and draft genome assembly. Plant J. 71, 895â906 (2012)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d1853e728_3">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Qi, J. et al. A genomic variation map provides insights into the genetic basis of cucumber domestication and diversity. Nat. Genet. 45, 1510â1515 (2013)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d1853e728_4">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Li, Q. et al. A chromosome-scale genome assembly of cucumber (Cucumis sativus L.). GigaScience 8, giz072 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d1853e731">17</a></sup>, melon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Garciamas, J. et al. The genome of melon (Cucumis melo L.). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. Plant Sci. 10, 1815 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d1853e738">20</a></sup>, zucchini<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Montero-Pau, J. et al. Draft genome sequence of bitter gourd (Momordica charantia), a vegetable and medicinal plant in tropical and subtropical regions. DNA Res. 24, 51â58 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d1853e773">30</a></sup>. However, no reference genome of snake gourd is available. Recent studies have shown that there is likely a common tetraploid ancestor of Cucurbitaceae species, and it has been inferred that cucurbits diverged from their common ancestor with grape 107â121 million years ago (Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Wang, J. et al. An overlooked paleotetraploidization in cucurbitaceae. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 16â26 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d1853e777">31</a></sup>. Watermelon is believed to have differentiated from within the Cucurbitaceae family ~20.4âMya and the divergence between melon and cucumber occurred 9.0â10.2âMya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Sun, H. et al. Karyotype stability and unbiased fractionation in the paleo-allotetraploid Cucurbita genomes. Mol. Plant 10, 1293â1306 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d1853e781">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Bellot, S. &amp; Renner, S. S. Exploring new dating approaches for parasites: the worldwide Apodanthaceae (Cucurbitales) as an example. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 80, 1â10 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d1853e784">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Schaefer, H., Heibl, C. &amp; Renner, S. S. Gourds afloat: a dated phylogeny reveals an Asian origin of the gourd family (Cucurbitaceae) and numerous oversea dispersal events. Proc. R. Soc. B 276, 843â851 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d1853e787">33</a></sup>. However, the evolutionary history of the snake gourd genome remains largely unexplored.</p><p>In this study, we assembled the genome of snake gourd using Nanopore long reads combined with Hi-C chromatin interaction maps. Protein-coding genes and noncoding RNAs (ncRNAs) were predicted from the genome assembly, after which the predicted genes were functionally annotated. Phylogenetic and comparative genomic analyses indicated that snake gourd is closely related to sponge gourd. Transcriptome analysis revealed candidate genes that are involved in fruit texture, pigment accumulation, plant hormones, and resistance, and the expression profiles during fruit development and ripening, which may contribute to the unique characteristics of snake gourd fruits. The genome sequence presented in this study provides insights into both the structural characteristics of the snake gourd genome and the evolutionary relationship of snake gourd and related species and serves as a valuable resource for genomic research and comparative genomics within the Cucurbitaceae.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly, anchoring, and quality evaluation</h3><p>For this study, snake gourd plants were grown in a greenhouse, as shown in Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig1">1a</a>. The immature fruits were greenish-white, long and slender (the length could reach 1.5â2âm). FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig1">1b</a> shows a longitudinal section of a snake gourd and the flesh of the immature fruit is white and tough. Two Illumina libraries with fragment sizes of ~350âbp were constructed for snake gourd and sequenced on the Illumina Novaseq 6000 sequencing platform (Illumina, 9885 Towne Centre Drive, San Diego, CA 92121, USA) in paired-end mode and with a read length of 150âbp. After cleaning, 65.5âGb of high-quality data were obtained, representing a 63.6-fold depth of the snake gourd genome, which has an estimated size of 1.03âGb based on the K-mer depth distribution analysis of the paired-end Illumina reads. The snake gourd genome is relatively large, highly homozygous, and estimated to contain 70.5% repetitive sequences, with a heterozygosity level of ~0.02% and a GC content of ~7.1%.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Panoramic and sectional view of snake gourd."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Panoramic and sectional view of snake gourd.</b></figcaption><div vegetable expo garden. <b>b</b> Longitudinal section of an immature snake gourd fruit</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A nanopore library was constructed and sequenced, generating a total of 135.9âGb of raw data. After cleaning, the data were reduced to 111.8âGb, representing ~108.5Ã the total data composing snake gourd genome, with an N50 read length of 32.7âkb. De novo assembly of the Nanopore reads resulted in an initial assembly of 919.8âMb consisting of 167 contigs with an N50 length of 21.9âMb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Tab1">1</a>). The single-base error rate in the genome sequence using Nanopore sequencing technology was 0.00156%. After error correction with Illumina paired-end and Hi-C interaction maps, we obtained a final assembly that was 919.8âMb in size comprising 202 contigs and 69 scaffolds, with a contig N50 of 20.11âMb and a scaffold N50 of 82.12âMb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Tab1">1</a>). A chromosome interaction heatmap was constructed (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig2">2a</a>), which showed a pattern consistent with that of the main Hi-C genome assemblies and provided confidence in the pseudomolecule construction. Based on the Hi-C contact maps, a total of 197 sequences covering ~918.8âMb (99.89% of the assembled genome) clustered into 11 groups that correspond to the 11 chromosomes of snake gourd (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig2">2b</a>), with the longest being 118.8âMb and the shortest being 64.0âMb (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#MOESM1">1</a> and TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Hi-C interaction heatmap of the snake gourd genome. The assembled genome was divided into 100âkb bins and valid interaction links of the Hi-C data were calculated between each pair of bins. The binary logarithm of the number of links in each bin was coded using colors ranging from light yellow to dark red, indicating the strength of Hi-C interaction links from low to high, respectively. LG01âLG11 represent the 11 chromosomes. <b>b</b> Landscape of the snake gourd genome. A: distribution of GC content (green); B: density of repeat sequences (blue); C: gene density (orange); D: noncoding RNA density (pink); E: syntenic blocks within the genome</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To evaluate the quality of the assembly, we mapped the cleaned Illumina short reads to the assembled genome using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d1853e1052">34</a></sup>, which had a mapping rate of 99.4%. Further evaluation using BUSCO (<a href="https://busco.ezlab.org/">https://busco.ezlab.org/</a>) with a database of 2,326 conserved core plant genes indicated that 95.7% of the core genes were found to be complete in the snake gourd genome assembly, including 93.5% that were single copies and 2.2% that were duplicated copies. In addition, 1.1% were found to be fragmented and only 3.2% were missing. Taken together, these results supported the integrity and high quality of the assembled snake gourd genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat sequence annotation and gene prediction</h3><p>Based on the principles of structure prediction and de novo prediction, we constructed a repeat sequence database of the snake gourd genome, which was used to screen the assembly for repeat sequences. A total of 736.1âMb (80.0%) of repetitive sequences were identified in the assembled genome. Among these repetitive sequences, long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons were predominant, constituting 66.7% of the snake gourd genome assembly, with 45.6% belonging to the copia type and 19.8% belonging to the gypsy type (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><p>Three prediction methodsâan ab initio strategy, a homology-based strategy, and an RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) strategyâwere used to predict protein-coding genes in the snake gourd genome and the predictions from these three methods were integrated using EVM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9, 1â22 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d1853e1077">35</a></sup> v1.1.1 software (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#MOESM1">1</a>). Ultimately, 22,874 protein-coding genes with a total length of 121.4âMb were predicted in the genome (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Tab2">2</a>) and were functionally annotated, the annotations of which are shown in Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#MOESM2">3</a>. Of the predicted genes, a total of 22,129 (96.74%) were annotated by five functional databases (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Tab2">2</a>). In addition, we also identified 74 miRNAs, 294 rRNAs, 1,167 tRNAs, and 3,021 pseudogenes.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Annotation statistics of the snake gourd genome assembly</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Evolution of the snake gourd genome</h3><p>To understand the evolution of the snake gourd genome, we collected genome sequences of representative plant species and performed a comparative genomic analysis with the genome sequence of snake gourd. Genes from 13 selected plant species, including 9 cucurbits (<i>T. anguina</i> L., <i>Luffa cylindrica</i> L., <i>Cucumis sativus</i> L., <i>Cucumis melo</i>, <i>Citrullus lanatus</i>, <i>Lagenaria siceraria</i>, <i>Cucurbita moschata</i>, <i>Cucurbita pepo</i>, and <i>Momordica charantia</i>), two rosid species (<i>Vitis vinifera</i> L. and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>), one monocot (<i>Oryza sativa</i>), and one in the basal lineage of angiosperms (<i>Amborella trichopoda</i>), clustered into 34,827 gene families. A total of 17,057 gene families were identified in snake gourd, 125 of which (comprising 451 genes) were specific to the snake gourd genome (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#MOESM1">4</a>). Clustering analysis revealed a total of 14,148 single-copy genes in snake gourd, accounting for 61.9% of the predicted genes, which is similar to that in other cucurbit species, such as <i>L. cylindrica</i>, <i>C. sativus</i>, <i>C. melo</i>, <i>C. lanatus</i>, <i>L. siceraria</i>, and <i>M. charantia</i>, in which no recent whole-genome duplication (WGD) events occurred, but is substantially higher than that in <i>C. moschata</i> and <i>C. pepo</i>, whose genomes underwent a recent WGD event (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig3">3a</a>). The clustering of gene families in snake gourd and several other cucurbit species, including sponge gourd, watermelon, bottle gourd, and pumpkin, is shown in Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig3">3b</a> and indicates that the number of snake gourd gene families (17,057) was most similar to the number of sponge gourd gene families (16,986).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Gene family, phylogenetic analysis, and Ks distribution of snake gourd and other representative plant species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Gene family, phylogenetic analysis, and Ks distribution of snake gourd and other representative plant species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00423-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_423_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00423-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_423_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="995" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Gene copy number distribution in snake gourd and 12 other plant species. <b>b</b> Venn diagram representing the clusters of gene families in snake gourd and four other related cucurbit species (sponge gourd, watermelon, bottle gourd, and pumpkin). <b>c</b> Phylogenetic tree of snake gourd and 12 other species based on a concatenated alignment of 970 single-copy protein sequences. The tree is rooted with <i>A. trichopoda</i> as the outgroup. CAFÃ-based estimates of gene family expansions and contractions. The red and blue numbers indicate expanded and contracted gene families, respectively. <b>d</b> Ks distribution in snake gourd and other representative plant species</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In addition, Gene Ontology (GO) and Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) enrichment analyses were performed for gene families specific to snake gourd. GO analysis showed that these families were enriched in genes involved in metabolic processes, cellular processes, and biological regulation or encoded products that have binding, nucleic acid binding, DNA binding, and catalytic activity function (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#MOESM1">2b</a>). KEGG pathway analysis indicated that genes related to flavonoid biosynthesis, regulation of autophagy, fatty acid degradation, etc., were enriched in the snake gourd-specific families (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#MOESM1">2a</a>). These included two genes (<i>EVM0012390.1</i> and <i>EVM0020640.1</i>) encoding flavonol synthase/flavanone 3-hydroxylase in the flavonoid biosynthetic pathway, two autophagy-related genes associated with the regulation of the autophagy pathway, and two genes encoding alcohol dehydrogenases (ADHs) (<i>EVM0001187.1</i> and <i>EVM0017122.1</i>), which are involved in fatty acid degradation, tyrosine metabolism, Î±-linolenic acid metabolism, and glycolysis/gluconeogenesis. Furthermore, a number of other genes were identified that are known to participate in fruit ripening, such as those encoding cell wall-associated hydrolase, phenylalanine ammonia-lyase (PAL) 1, malonateâCoA ligase, and aquaporin PIP2-2. Together, these specific genes and gene families may contribute to the unique fruit features of snake gourd (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Tab3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Select gene annotations for specific gene families in snake gourd</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Based on 970 single-copy genes in snake gourd and in 12 other plant species, a phylogenetic tree was constructed. The phylogenetic tree showed that, of all the Cucurbitaceae species examined, snake gourd is most closely related to sponge gourd (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig3">3c</a>). Snake gourd and sponge gourd diverged from their common ancestor approximately 33â47âMya after the divergence from the common ancestor together with bitter gourd (44â87âMya) but before the divergence between watermelon and bottle gourd (14â27âMya) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig3">3c</a>). In addition, we performed a comparative analysis of the evolution of gene families in the 13 plant species. In snake gourd, 226 gene families comprising 1406 genes exhibited significant expansion (<i>p</i>â&lt;â0.01) relative to those of the last common ancestor, whereas 268 gene families showed contraction (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig3">3c</a>). KEGG and GO functional analyses showed that most of the members of the expanded snake gourd gene families are involved in metabolic processes, cellular processes, cell catalytic activity, binding, flavonoid biosynthesis, regulation of autophagy, fatty acid degradation, and tyrosine metabolism (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#MOESM1">3</a>), and included genes encoding cellulose synthase, wall-associated receptor kinase, exopolygalacturonase, polygalacturonase (PG), lipoxygenase, linoleate 9S-lipoxygenase 6, terpene synthase, (â)-germacrene <span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-synthase, (+)-Î³-cadinene synthase, myrcene synthase, 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylate oxidase (ACO), auxin-induced protein 22D, 23âkDa jasmonate-induced protein, anthocyanidin 3-<i>O</i>-glucosyltransferase, and flavonoid hydroxylase. These genes are involved in cell wall biosynthesis and degradation, flavor and aromatic compound generation, phytohormone synthesis and signal transduction, and flavonoid biosynthesis. Other gene families involved in plant defense and response to pathogens were also found to be expanded in snake gourd, such as those whose members encode UDP-glucosyltransferase, salicylate carboxymethyltransferase, tobacco mosaic virus (TMV) resistance protein N, and other disease resistance proteins.</p><p>WGD events have occurred widely in angiosperms and many plant species have experienced genome duplications in their evolutionary history, which are of great significance in understanding speciation, genome evolution, and gene neofunctionalization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Van de Peer, Y., Maere, S. &amp; Meyer, A. The evolutionary significance of ancient genome duplications. Nat. Rev. Genet. 10, 725â732 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d1853e1897">36</a></sup>. However, our in-depth genomic analysis indicated that no recent WGD events have occurred in the snake gourd genome, which is consistent with the findings of a recent report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Guo, J. et al. Phylotranscriptomics in Cucurbitaceae reveal multiple whole genome duplications and key morphological and molecular innovations. Mol. Plant 13, 1117â1133 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d1853e1901">37</a></sup>. The Ks distribution among these species suggested that snake gourd diverged from sponge gourd ~42âMya and from watermelon ~47âMya (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig3">3d</a>). A peak centering on a Ks of ~1.32 was observed between snake gourd paralogous pairs (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig3">3d</a>), which corresponds to the ancient whole-genome triplication (the Î³ event) shared by all core eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Wang, J. et al. An overlooked paleotetraploidization in cucurbitaceae. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 16â26 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d1853e1911">31</a></sup>. In addition, genome collinearity analysis between snake gourd, sponge gourd, and watermelon (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#MOESM1">4</a>) showed a high degree of gene-order conservation with the snake gourd genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Transcriptomes at different ripening stages of snake gourd fruits</h3><p>To identify potential candidate genes related to fruit characteristics and quality during fruit development and ripening, transcriptome analysis was used to study changes in differentially expressed genes (DEGs) in the fruits at different stages. The results showed that there were 362 common DEGs in the development and ripening stages (20 d vs. 40 d and 40 d vs. 60 d) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig4">4a</a>). Among them, five genes related to disease resistance and defense responses overlapped between the two groups, <i>leucine-rich repeat receptor-like protein kinase TDR</i>, <i>LRR receptor-like serine/threonine-protein kinase FEI 1</i>, <i>TMV resistance protein N</i>, <i>leucine-rich repeat extensin-like protein 6</i>, and <i>respiratory burst oxidase homolog protein E</i>, all of which participate in the plantâpathogen interaction pathway (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#MOESM3">5</a>). These genes were upregulated at 20 d vs. 40 d and downregulated at 40 d vs. 60 d, which may be related to changes in fruit resistance during ripening.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Differentially expressed genes (DEGs) at different stages of snake gourd fruit and changes in transcription during fruit development and ripening."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Differentially expressed genes (DEGs) at different stages of snake gourd fruit and changes in transcription during fruit development and ripening.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00423-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_423_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00423-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_423_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="964" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Venn diagram representing the number of DEGs between the 20 d vs. 40 d and 40 d vs. 60 d groups; 362 DEGs overlapped between the two groups. <b>b</b> Upregulated KEGG pathways in the 20 d vs. 40 d group comparison. <b>c</b> Upregulated GO terms related to biological processes in the 20 d vs. 40 d group comparison. <b>d</b> Netplot of significantly enriched GO terms in the 20 d vs. 40 d group comparison. <b>e</b> Upregulated KEGG pathways in the 40 d vs. 60 d group comparison</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>During the period of fruit development, a total of 480 genes, including 356 (74%) upregulated and 124 (26%) downregulated genes, were differentially expressed in the 20 d vs. 40 d comparison. KEGG pathway analysis of the upregulated genes showed that they were mainly enriched in the pathways involved in phenylpropanoid biosynthesis, amino sugar and nucleotide sugar metabolism, protein processing in the endoplasmic reticulum and starch and sucrose metabolism (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig4">4b</a>). The expression of several peroxidase genes, such as <i>peroxidase 31</i> (increase of 3.4-fold), <i>peroxidase 55-like</i> (increase of 4.0-fold), <i>peroxidase 5</i> (increase of 6.4-fold), <i>peroxidase 64-like</i> (increase of 8.8-fold), <i>peroxidase 2-like</i> (increase of 11.6-fold), <i>peroxidase 21-like</i> (increase of 10.5-fold), and <i>probable mannitol dehydrogenase</i> (increase of 2.1-fold), markedly changed in snake gourd fruit (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#MOESM3">5</a>). Of the upregulated GO terms, the most enriched categories related to biological processes included the xyloglucan metabolic process, cell wall biogenesis, the carbohydrate metabolic process, and cell wall organization (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig4">4c</a>). The corresponding gene networks associated with significantly abundant GO items in the 20 d vs. 40 d group are shown in Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig4">4d</a>. Many enzymes related to cell wall metabolism were identified as being upregulated, including Î²-glucosidase (BGLU) 18-like, Î²-galactosidase (GAL) 5-like/10, pectinesterase-like (PE), expansin-A4/A10-like (EXP), endoglucanase (EG) 10/11/17-like, glucan endo-1,3-Î²-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-glucosidase, and glucan endo-1,3-Î²-glucosidase 13-like but not cellulose synthase-like protein H1. In addition, increased expression of plant hormone-related genes was also found, including <i>abscisic acid receptor PYR1/PYL4-like</i>, <i>auxin-induced protein 22D-like</i>, and <i>auxin-responsive protein IAA16/IAA21-like/IAA29-like</i>. It is generally known that the phytohormone auxin can regulate cell growth and induce tracheary element differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Fukuda, H. Tracheary element differentiation. Plant Cell 9, 1147â1156 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2033">38</a></sup>.</p><p>We identified 4801 genes that were differentially expressed by comparing the transcriptome at 60 d with that at 40 d. A total of 1957 (41%) genes were upregulated and 2844 (59%) genes were downregulated. In specific gene families of snake gourd, five genes were found to be differentially expressed: <i>PAL</i>, <i>cysteine protein inhibitor 1-like</i>, <i>MLP-like protein 329</i>, <i>poly transporter 5-like</i>, and <i>PAL 3</i>. Their expression profiles were found to be downregulated after 60 d compared with 40 d. The results of the KEGG pathway analysis of the upregulated genes suggested that the most enriched pathways were involved in fatty acid biosynthesis, carbon metabolism, pyruvate metabolism, and carotenoid biosynthesis (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig4">4e</a>). Among the 15 genes involved in carotenoid synthesis, 10 were upregulated, including <i>phytoene synthase</i> (<i>PSY</i>) (increase of 1.7-fold), <i>15-cis-phytoene desaturase</i> (increase of 4.6-fold), <i>15-cis-zeta-carotene isomerase</i> (increase of 5.1-fold), <i>zeta-carotene desaturase</i> (<i>ZDS</i>) (increase of 19.0-fold), <i>prolycopene isomerase</i> (<i>CRTISO</i>) (increase of 25-fold), and <i>Î²-carotene 3-hydroxylase 1</i> (increase of 40.0-fold), and 5 were downregulated, including <i>lycopene epsilon cyclase</i>, <i>lycopene Î²-cyclase</i>, <i>probable 9-cis-epoxycarotenoid dioxygenase NCED5</i>, and <i>abscisic acid 8-hydroxylase 4-like</i>. Moreover, 11 genes related to the accumulation of flavonoids and anthocyanidins in the fruits were also found, including <i>anthocyanidin 3-O-glucosyltransferase 2-like</i>, <i>flavonoid 3-monooxygenase-like</i>, <i>flavonoid 3</i>,<i>5-methyltransferase-like</i>, <i>flavonol synthase/flavanone 3-hydroxylase-like</i>, and <i>isoflavone reductase-like protein</i>, which exhibited biphasic expression patterns. <i>PAL</i> is involved in the phenylalanine metabolism pathway and functions prior to lignin synthesis, and the expression of many <i>peroxidases</i> related to enzymes associated with lignification, the cell wall, auxin catabolism, defense against pathogens, and scavenging of reactive oxygen species is downregulated. In addition, the expression levels of numerous genes related to plant hormones synthesis and signal transduction were significantly altered: the levels of <i>ACO homolog 1/3-like</i>, <i>ethylene receptor 2-like</i>, <i>ethylene response sensor 1</i>, the <i>serine/threonine-protein kinase CTR1</i>, <i>ethylene insensitive 3-like 3 protein</i>, <i>auxin response factor 18</i> (<i>ARF</i>), <i>auxin-responsive protein SAUR71-like</i>, <i>gibberellin-regulated protein 1-like</i> (<i>GRP</i>), and <i>indole-3-acetic acid-amido synthetase GH3.17</i> were upregulated, and the expression levels of others, such as <i>ACO1/3</i>, <i>ethylene-responsive transcription factor 1B-like</i> (<i>ERF</i>), <i>indole-3-acetic acid-amido synthetase GH3.10/GH3.6-like</i>, <i>auxin-induced protein 15A/22D/22B-like</i>, <i>ARF 4/9/18</i>, <i>auxin-responsive protein IAA/SAUR50-like</i>, <i>auxin transporter-like protein 1/2</i>, the <i>jasmonic acid-amido synthetase JAR1</i>, <i>abscisic acid receptor PYR1-like</i>, <i>GRP 9/4-like</i>, and the <i>AP2-like ethylene-responsive transcription factor ANT</i>, were downregulated. Furthermore, the mRNAs for some enzymes related to fruit cell wall structure also changed significantly, with <i>PE</i>, <i>PE 3-like</i>, <i>pectate lyase 5/8/12/18</i> (<i>PL</i>), <i>pectin acetylesterase 8-like</i>, <i>PG-like</i>, <i>PG At1g48100-like</i>, <i>PG QRT3-like</i>, <i>GAL 3/10/5-like</i>, <i>BGLU 18/44-like</i>, <i>glucan endo-1</i>,<i>3-Î²-glucosidase</i>, <i>expansin</i> (<i>EXP</i>), <i>EG 6/10/11/17/24-like</i>, and <i>cellulose synthase-like protein G3</i> downregulated while others such as <i>Î²-</i><i><span class="u-small-caps">d</span></i><i>-xylosidase1-like</i>, <i>GAL 13-like</i>, <i>GAL 16</i>, <i>GAL-like</i>, <i>cellulose synthase-like protein E1/H1</i>, and <i>EG 9-like</i> were downregulated. These changes in mRNAs of cell wall enzymes may be related to fruit texture changes, as the fruits were softer after 60 d compared with 40 d.</p><p>We focused our subsequent analysis on cell wall-related gene families whose members are responsible for fruit texture in snake gourd. The glycosyl hydrolase family (GH) related to cell wall structure was selected to construct a phylogenetic tree. The GH families were divided into four subfamilies: GH-1, GH-9, GH-28, and GH-35. The members of the GH-28 and GH-35 subfamilies encode the majority of GH proteins. The genes belonging to the GH-28 and GH-35 subfamilies in snake gourd showed the closest phylogenetic relationships with those of pumpkin and sponge gourd (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig5">5a</a>). In the snake gourd genome, the GH-1 subfamily contained five members, but only one, one, and three proteins belonged to the GH-1 subfamily in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, pumpkin, and sponge gourd, respectively; moreover, there were no proteins that clustered within the GH-9 subfamily in these three plant species (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig5">5a</a>). Based on the transcriptome data, we further analyzed the expression of 15 DEGs related to cell wall modification and pigment accumulation in the 20 d vs. 40 d (SG1 vs. SG2) and 40 d vs. 60 d (SG2 vs. SG3) group comparisons, respectively. A heatmap of these DEGs showed the gene expression profiles at different stages (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig5">5b, c</a>). In the 20 d vs. 40 d group comparison (SG1 vs. SG2), numerous members of the GH family were highly expressed at this fruit development stage, with the exception of PE (EVM0004218), and the results of the three replications were similar (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig5">5c</a>). In the 40 d vs. 60 d group comparison (SG2 vs. SG3), ZDS (EVM0002371), prolycopene isomerase (EVM0010946), and 9-<i>cis</i>-epoxycarotenoid dioxygenase NCED2 (EVM0012548) had much higher expression in SG3, whereas the expression levels of abscisic acid 8-hydroxylase 4-like (EVM0017015 and EVM0004622) and 9-<i>cis</i>-epoxycarotenoid dioxygenase NCED5 (EVM0015273) decreased during fruit maturation (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Fig5">5c</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Phylogenetic tree of glycosyl hydrolase families (GHs) and a heatmap, with replicates, of differentially expressed genes (DEGs) at different stages in snake gourd fruits."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5:
