of fruit pigment accumulation-related genes at the 40 d (SG2) and 60 d (SG3) stages. The red and green colors indicate high and low expression levels, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Snake gourd is an excellent vegetable species that is popular for both its unique flavor and its nutritional, health, medicinal and ornamental value and has high utilization potential<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Devi, N. Medicinal values of Trichosanthus cucumerina L. (snake gourd) - a review. Br. J. Pharm. Res. 16, 1â10 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2340">2</a></sup>. However, research on this species is limited<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adebooye, O. C. Phytoconstituents and anti-oxidant activity of the pulp of snake tomato (Tricosanthes cucumerina). Afr. J. Tradit. Complement. Altern. Med. 5, 173â179 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2344">3</a></sup>, and related molecular-level studies are scarce. We aimed to sequence, assemble, annotate, and comparatively analyze the snake gourd genome to provide a basis for future investigations and improvement of this species. This is the first report of a high-quality genome for snake gourd, which has one of the largest genomes of Cucurbitaceae species sequenced to date.</p><p>The assembled genome size is ~919.76âMb, which is close to that of wax gourd (913âMb) but larger than those of other Cucurbitaceae species, such as watermelon, melon, pumpkin, bottle gourd, cucumber, zucchini, bitter gourd (200â400âMb), and sponge gourd (669âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, Q. et al. A chromosome-scale genome assembly of cucumber (Cucumis sativus L.). GigaScience 8, giz072 (2019)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2351">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Garciamas, J. et al. The genome of melon (Cucumis melo L.). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 11872â11877 (2012)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2351_1">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, H. et al. A high-quality melon genome assembly provides insights into genetic basis of fruit trait improvement. iScience 22, 16â27 (2019)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2351_2">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Castanera, R., Ruggieri, V., Pujol, M., Garcia-Mas, J. &amp; Casacuberta, J. M. An improved melon reference genome with single-molecule sequencing uncovers a recent burst of transposable elements with potential impact on genes. Front. Plant Sci. 10, 1815 (2020)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2351_3">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Montero-Pau, J. et al. De novo assembly of the zucchini genome reveals a whole-genome duplication associated with the origin of the Cucurbita genus. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1161â1171 (2018)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2351_4">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wu, S. et al. The bottle gourd genome provides insights into Cucurbitaceae evolution and facilitates mapping of a Papaya ring-spot virus resistance locus. Plant J. 92, 963â975 (2017)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2351_5">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Guo, S. et al. The draft genome of watermelon (Citrullus lanatus) and resequencing of 20 diverse accessions. Nat. Genet. 45, 51â58 (2013)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2351_6">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Guo, S. et al. Resequencing of 414 cultivated and wild watermelon accessions identifies selection for fruit quality traits. Nat. Genet. 51, 1616â1623 (2019)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2351_7">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wu, S. et al. Genome of âCharleston Grayâ, the principal American watermelon cultivar, and genetic characterization of 1,365 accessions in the U.S. National Plant Germplasm System watermelon collection. Plant Biotechnol. J. 17, 2246â2258 (2019)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2351_8">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sun, H. et al. Karyotype stability and unbiased fractionation in the paleo-allotetraploid Cucurbita genomes. Mol. Plant 10, 1293â1306 (2017)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2351_9">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Barrera-Redondo, J. et al. The genome of Cucurbita argyrosperma (Silver-Seed Gourd) reveals faster rates of protein-coding gene and long noncoding RNA turnover and neofunctionalization within Cucurbita. Mol. Plant 12, 506â520 (2019)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2351_10">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xie, D. et al. The wax gourd genomes offer insights into the genetic diversity and ancestral cucurbit karyotype. Nat. Commun. 10, 5158 (2019)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2351_11">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, T. et al. Long-read sequencing and de novo assembly of the sponge gourd cylindrica (L.) Roem. Genome Mol. Ecol. Resour. 20, 511â519 (2020)." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2351_12">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Urasaki, N. et al. Draft genome sequence of bitter gourd (Momordica charantia), a vegetable and medicinal plant in tropical and subtropical regions. DNA Res. 24, 51â58 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2354">30</a></sup> (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Tab4">4</a>). We predicted 22,874 protein-coding genes, which is similar to the number of genes present in melon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Zhang, H. et al. A high-quality melon genome assembly provides insights into genetic basis of fruit trait improvement. iScience 22, 16â27 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2361">19</a></sup>, slightly more than the number in bottle gourd<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Wu, S. et al. The bottle gourd genome provides insights into Cucurbitaceae evolution and facilitates mapping of a Papaya ring-spot virus resistance locus. Plant J. 92, 963â975 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2365">22</a></sup>, but less than the number in the eleven other Cucurbitaceae species examined (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Tab4">4</a>). Repetitive sequences such as transposable elements play crucial roles in genome evolution, chromosome rearrangement, and gene regulation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Heitz, T., Bergey, D. R. &amp; Ryan, C. A. A gene encoding a chloroplast-targeted lipoxygenase in tomato leaves is transiently induced by-wounding, systemin, and methyl jasmonate. Plant Physiol. 114, 1085â1093 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d1853e2373">39</a></sup> but have presented a major challenge for producing high-quality genome assemblies. We found that 80.0% of the snake gourd genome is composed of repeated sequences, with LTRs accounting for 66.7% of the genome, which is quite high compared with the percentage of repeat content in other Cucurbitaceae genomes. BUSCO (<a href="https://busco.ezlab.org/">https://busco.ezlab.org/</a>)-based assessment revealed that the snake gourd assembly is more complete than or comparable to other reported cucurbit reference genomes (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#Tab4">4</a>) and provides valuable information about evolutionary events and gene family expansion in snake gourd; in addition, this assembly serves as a useful resource for comparative genomic studies in Cucurbitaceae.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 Comparison of the snake gourd genome assembly and other Cucurbitaceae genome assemblies</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In specific gene families of snake gourd, a number of genes have been found to potentially participate in fruit ripening, such as several genes encoding autophagy-related proteins, ADHs, and the aquaporin PIP2-2. Autophagy is involved in plant processes such as floret ripening (in wheat)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Ghiglione, H. O. et al. Autophagy regulated by day length determines the number of fertile florets in wheat. Plant J. 55, 1010â1024 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3141">40</a></sup>, root tip cell growth and differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Yano, K., Suzuki, T. &amp; Moriyasu, Y. Constitutive autophagy in plant root cells. Autophagy 3, 360â362 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3145">41</a></sup>, and chloroplast degradation in senescing leaves<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Izumi, M., Wada, S., Makino, A. &amp; Ishida, H. The autophagic degradation of chloroplasts via rubisco-containing bodies is specifically linked to leaf carbon status but not nitrogen status in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 154, 1196â1209 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3149">42</a></sup>. The autophagy pathway has also been reported to be involved in plant innate immunity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Wang, Y., Nishimura, M. T., Zhao, T. &amp; Tang, D. ATG2, an autophagy-related protein, negatively affects powdery mildew resistance and mildew-induced cell death in Arabidopsis. Plant J. 68, 74â87 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3153">43</a></sup> and responses to a variety of abiotic stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Liu, Y., Xiong, Y. &amp; Bassham, D. C. Autophagy is required for tolerance of drought and salt stress in plants. Autophagy 5, 954â963 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3157">44</a></sup>, and in assisting plants in surviving nutrient-limitation stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Aubert, S. et al. Ultrastructural and biochemical characterization of autophagy in higher plant cells subjected to carbon deprivation: control by the supply of mitochondria with respiratory substrates. J. Cell Biol. 133, 1251â1263 (1996)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3162">45</a></sup>. ADH enzymes play multiple roles in anaerobic fermentation, aerobic fermentation, and the production of scents that discourage predation, attract pollinators, and facilitate seed dispersal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Strommer, J. The plant ADH gene family. Plant J. 66, 128â142 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3166">46</a></sup>. In petunia, both the <i>ADH2</i> and <i>ADH3</i> genes act in the lipoxygenase pathway to produce floral scents<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Garabagi, F., Duns, G. &amp; Strommer, J. Selective recruitment of Adh genes for distinct enzymatic functions in Petunia hybrida. Plant Mol. Biol. 58, 283â294 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3176">47</a></sup> and flavor volatiles in ripening fruits. A previous study reported a strawberry fruit-specific aquaporin, FaPIP1;1, which showed an expression profile associated with fruit ripening<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Alleva, K. et al. Cloning, functional characterization, and co-expression studies of a novel aquaporin (FaPIP2;1) of strawberry fruit. J. Exp. Bot. 61, 3935â3945 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3180">48</a></sup>. Further study indicated that the FaPIP aquaporins showed an expression pattern associated with fruit firmness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Alleva, K. et al. Cloning, functional characterization, and co-expression studies of a novel aquaporin (FaPIP2;1) of strawberry fruit. J. Exp. Bot. 61, 3935â3945 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3185">48</a></sup>. In addition, several specific genes were found to be differentially expressed between 40 d and 60 d, which may contribute to the unique snake gourd fruit features.</p><p>Between 20 d and 40 d, the snake gourd fruits became longer and harder, and we detected increased transcript levels of genes related to cell division, cell expansion, fruit growth, and protection of the fruit surface<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Gillaspy, G. E., BenDavid, H. &amp; Gruissem, W. Fruits: a developmental perspective. Plant Cell 5, 1439â1451 (1993)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3192">49</a></sup>. We identified transcripts for highly expressed cell wall-modifying genes similar to those expressed in expanding cucumber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Ando, K., Carr, K. M. &amp; Grumet, R. Transcriptome analyses of early cucumber fruit growth identifies distinct gene modules associated with phases of development. BMC Genomics 13, 518 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3196">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Ando, K. &amp; Grumet, R. Transcriptional profiling of rapidly growing cucumber fruit by 454-pyrosequencing analysis. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 135, 291â302 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3199">51</a></sup>, tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Lemaire-Chamley, M. et al. Changes in transcriptional profiles are associated with early fruit tissue specialization in tomato. Plant Physiol. 139, 750â769 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3203">52</a></sup>, melon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Mascarell-Creus, A. et al. An oligo-based microarray offers novel transcriptomic approaches for the analysis of pathogen resistance and fruit quality traits in melon (Cucumis melo L.). BMC Genomics 10, 467 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3207">53</a></sup>, and watermelon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Wechter, W. P. et al. Gene expression in developing watermelon fruit. BMC Genomics 9, 275 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3211">54</a></sup> fruits, such as <i>EXP</i>s, <i>endo-1</i>,<i>2-B-glucanase</i>, <i>BGLU</i>s, <i>PL</i>s, and <i>pectin methylesterases</i>. Major increases in transcripts for mRNAs encoding enzymes related to cell wall metabolism were detected, including <i>GAL 10</i> (increase of 14.3-fold), <i>PE-like</i> (increase of 21.6-fold), <i>EG 17-like</i> (increase of 21.2-fold), <i>expansin-A10-like</i> (increase of 27.1-fold), <i>UDP-glucuronate 4-epimerase 1-like</i> (increase of 29.8-fold), and <i>PE 53</i> (increase of 30.4-fold), which may be involved in the production of longer and harder fruits. We also detected the upregulated expression of auxin-related genes. These genes may be involved in fruit elongation, as many studies have shown that phytohormones, especially auxin and gibberellins, are related to fruit growth and changes in fruit shape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Wang, Y. P. et al. A comparison of sun, ovate, fs8.1 and auxin application on tomato fruit shape and gene expression. Plant Cell Physiol. 60, 1067â1081 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3254">55</a></sup>. These results are consistent with those of previous studies that found that three homologs of auxin-related genes were differentially expressed in developing watermelon fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Wechter, W. P. et al. Gene expression in developing watermelon fruit. BMC Genomics 9, 275 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3258">54</a></sup>. In addition, we found an increase in resistance (R) gene transcripts, such as those of <i>TMV resistance protein N</i>, <i>leucine-rich repeat extensin-like protein 3</i>, and <i>LRR receptor-like serine/threonine-protein kinase FEI 1</i>. Studies have shown that younger and smaller fruits are more susceptible to infection than older and larger fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Gevens, A. J., Ando, K., Lamour, K. H., Grumet, R. &amp; Hausbeck, M. K. A detached cucumber fruit method to screen for resistance to Phytophthora capsici and effect of fruit age on susceptibility to infection. Plant Dis. 90, 1276â1282 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3271">56</a></sup>, and changes in the expression of resistance genes may explain the stronger resistance of larger fruits compared with smaller ones. These genes mainly regulate the resistance of plants to pathogens and insects, and in cucumber, eight DEGs have been identified that may be associated with aphid resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Liang, D. N. et al. Identification of differentially expressed genes related to aphid resistance in cucumber (Cucumis sativus L.). Sci. Rep. 5, 9645 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3276">57</a></sup>.</p><p>Transcripts for several enzymes mentioned above related to cell wall metabolism were found to be downregulated during ripening from 40 to 60 d, which may be the cause of fruit softening at 60 d. The expression levels of these enzymes are different among various fruits and at different fruit developmental stages. Studies on watermelon have shown that there are differences in the expression of genes related to cell wall components in two kinds of flesh with different textures<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Gao, Y. et al. Transcriptome analysis of genes related to fruit texture in watermelon. Sci. Hortic. 262, 109075 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3284">58</a></sup>, and the key genes involved in the regulation of flesh texture were differentially expressed in cultivated and wild watermelon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Guo, S. G. et al. Comparative transcriptome analysis of cultivated and wild watermelon during fruit development. PLoS ONE 10, e0130267 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3288">59</a></sup>. In addition, the genes involved in the upstream and downstream steps of the carotenoid biosynthesis or degradation pathway were upregulated and downregulated, respectively. These changes may contribute to the increased carotenoid accumulation, making the fruit turn orange after ripening. This is supported by research on carotenoid accumulation and related gene expression during squash fruit development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Nakkanong, K., Yang, J. H. &amp; Zhang, M. F. Carotenoid accumulation and carotenogenic gene expression during fruit development in novel interspecific inbred squash lines and their parents. J. Agric. Food Chem. 60, 5936â5944 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3292">60</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Snake gourd sample collection and genome sequencing</h3><p>Young fresh leaf samples were collected from snake gourd plants grown in the greenhouse of the Shouguang vegetable expo garden. Genomic DNA was extracted from young leaves and used to construct two Illumina DNA libraries whose fragment size was ~350âbp, according to the standard protocols provided by the Illumina company. The libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq X platform in paired-end mode and with read length of 150âbp. The sequence data were evaluated (GC distribution statistics and quality value assessment) and filtered to obtain high-quality clean reads, which were subsequently used for estimation of the genome size, GC content, heterozygosity level, and postassembly error correction and evaluation. The raw sequencing reads were processed for quality control and for the removal of adapter and low-quality sequences using a custom Perl script. After trimming, reads with lengths less than 100âbp or a Q30 value &lt;â85% were discarded.</p><p>A Nanopore library was constructed from high-molecular-weight DNA using a Ligation Sequencing Kit (SQK-LSK109) and sequenced on a PromethION R9 flow cell with a PromethION Flow Cell Priming Kit (EXP-FLP001.PRO.6) (Oxford Nanopore Technologies, UK) according to the manufacturerâs instructions. The Oxford Nanopore reads were self-corrected using Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3311">61</a></sup> and the corrected reads were assembled into contigs using SMARTdenovo (<a href="https://github.com/ruanjue/smartdenovo">https://github.com/ruanjue/smartdenovo</a>). The assembled contigs were further polished using the Racon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Vaser, R., Sovic, I., Nagarajan, N. &amp; Sikic, M. Fast and accurate de novo genome assembly from long uncorrected reads. Genome Res. 27, 737â746 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3322">62</a></sup> and Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3326">63</a></sup> programs in conjunction with cleaned Illumina reads.</p><p>We also constructed Hi-C fragment libraries (insert size of 300â700âbp) and sequenced them using the Illumina platform. The raw Hi-C reads were processed to trim adapter sequences and to remove low-quality reads. The cleaned Hi-C reads were first truncated at the putative Hi-C junctions, and then the resulting trimmed reads were aligned to the genome assembly with BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3333">34</a></sup> (version 0.7.10-r789). Only uniquely aligned paired-end reads with a mapping quality greater than 20 were used for further analysis. Invalid read pairs including dangling-end and self-cycle, religation and dumped products were filtered by HiC-Pro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Servant, N. et al. HiC-Pro: an optimized and flexible pipeline for Hi-C data processing. Genome Biol. 16, 259 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3337">64</a></sup> v2.8.1. Approximately 75% of the uniquely mapped read pairs were valid interaction pairs and were used for correction of the assembled contigs, which were then clustered, ordered, and oriented onto chromosomes by LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3341">65</a></sup> with the parameters âCLUSTER_MIN_RE_SITES, 52; CLUSTER_MAX_LINK_DENSITY, 2; ORDER_MIN_N_RES_IN_TRUN, 51; ORDER_MIN_N_RES_IN_SHREDS, 50.â</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec10">Gene prediction and functional annotation</h3><p>A repetitive sequence database of the snake gourd genome was constructed using LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3353">66</a></sup> and RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, 351â358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3357">67</a></sup>. Repeat sequences in the database were classified using PASTEClassifier<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Hoede, C. et al. PASTEC: An Automatic Transposable Element Classification Tool. PLoS ONE 9, 106 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3361">68</a></sup> and then merged together with the repeats in RepBase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3365">69</a></sup> to construct the final repetitive sequence database. Repetitive sequences of the snake gourd genome were predicted using RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 25 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3369">70</a></sup> based on the constructed repetitive sequence database.</p><p>Protein-coding genes were predicted from the snake gourd genome using three different strategies: ab initio prediction, homology-based prediction, and transcript-based prediction. We used GENSCAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol. 268, 78â94 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3376">71</a></sup>, Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19, 215â225 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3380">72</a></sup> v2.4, GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3384">73</a></sup> v3.0.4, GeneID<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Blanco, E., Parra, G. &amp; GuigÃ³, R. Using geneid to identify genes. Curr. Protoc. Hum. Genet. 64, e56 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3388">74</a></sup> v1.4, and SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3392">75</a></sup> (version 2006-07-28) for ab initio predictions, and GeMoMa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Keilwagen, J., Hartung, F., Paulini, M., Twardziok, S. &amp; Grau, J. Combining RNA-seq data and homology-based gene prediction for plants, animals and fungi. BMC Bioinformatics 19, 1â12 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3397">76</a></sup> v1.3.1 was used for homology-based predictions using protein sequences from <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. lanatus</i>, <i>C. sativus</i>, and <i>L. cylindrica</i>. For transcript-based predictions, a mixture of four tissues (root, stem, leaf and fruit tissues) of snake gourd was used to construct an Illumina RNA-Seq library, which was subsequently subjected to PE (2âÃâ150âbp) sequencing on an Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform (Illumina, San Diego, CA, USA). After discarding the reads with low-quality bases, adapter sequences, and duplicated sequences, the retained high-quality clean reads were mapped to the snake gourd genome assembly using HISAT2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Kim, D., Paggi, J. M., Park, C., Bennett, C. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Graph-based genome alignment and genotyping with HISAT2 and HISAT-genotype. Nat. Biotechnol. 37, 907â915 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3413">77</a></sup> v2.0.4 and, based on the mapping results, the reads were assembled into transcripts using StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290â295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3418">78</a></sup> v1.2.3, TransDecoder v2.0 (<a href="http://transdecoder.github.io">http://transdecoder.github.io</a>) and GeneMarkS-T<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Tang, S., Lomsadze, A. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Identification of protein coding regions in RNA transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, e78 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3429">79</a></sup> v5.1; the assembled transcripts were subsequently used for gene prediction. RNA-Seq reads were also de novo-assembled using Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644â652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3433">80</a></sup> and PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Campbell, M. A., Haas, B. J., Hamilton, J. P., Mount, S. M. &amp; Buell, C. R. Comprehensive analysis of alternative splicing in rice and comparative analyses with Arabidopsis. BMC Genomics 7, 327 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3437">81</a></sup> v2.0.2 was used for gene prediction from these de novo<i>-</i>assembled transcripts. Finally, EVM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9, 1â22 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3445">35</a></sup> v1.1.1 was used to integrate the prediction results obtained by the above three methods. In addition, we also predicted different ncRNAs. MicroRNA and rRNA sequences were predicted based on the comparison of the snake gourd genome to the content of the Rfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Griffiths-Jones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 121â124 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3449">82</a></sup> database via BLASTn and tRNA sequences were predicted using tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 0955â0964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3453">83</a></sup>. Pseudogene prediction was also performed. Using the predicted protein sequence, through a BLAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Kent, W. J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3457">84</a></sup> comparison, we identified possible homologous gene sequences in the genome and then used GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3461">85</a></sup> to identify premature termination codons and frameshift mutations in the gene sequences to identify pseudogenes.</p><p>The protein sequences of the snake gourd genes were compared to the content of the NCBI nr<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Marchler-Bauer, A. et al. CDD: a Conserved Domain Database for the functional annotation of proteins. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, 225â229 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3468">86</a></sup>, KOG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Koonin, E. V. et al. A comprehensive evolutionary classification of proteins encoded in complete eukaryotic genomes. Genome Biol. 5, 1â28 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3472">87</a></sup>, GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Dimmer, E. C. et al. The UniProt-GO annotation database in 2011. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, 565â570 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3476">88</a></sup>, KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27â30 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3480">89</a></sup>, and TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365â370 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3484">90</a></sup> databases using BLAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403â410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3489">91</a></sup> v2.2.31, with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>, and functional annotations of the snake gourd genes were derived from the homologous sequences in these databases.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Comparative genomic analysis</h3><p>The protein sequences from snake gourd and 12 other representative plant species were clustered into orthologous groups using OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: phylogenetic orthology inference for comparative genomics. Genome Biol. 20, 1â14 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3506">92</a></sup> v2.3.7. The obtained orthologous groups (gene families) were annotated using the PANTHER v15 database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Mi, H., Muruganujan, A., Ebert, D., Huang, X. &amp; Thomas, P. D. PANTHER version 14: more genomes, a new PANTHER GO-slim and improvements in enrichment analysis tools. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, 419â426 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3510">93</a></sup>. GO and KEGG enrichment analyses of gene families unique to snake gourd were ultimately performed using ClusterProfile<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Yu, G., Wang, L. G., Han, Y. &amp; He, Q. Y. clusterProfiler: an R package for comparing biological themes among gene clusters. Omics 16, 284â287 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3514">94</a></sup> v3.14.0.</p><p>By the use of 970 single-copy protein sequences, an evolutionary tree was constructed using the maximum likelihood method implemented in IQ-TREE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Nguyen, L. T., Schmidt, H. A., Von Haeseler, A. &amp; Minh, B. Q. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268â274 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3521">95</a></sup> v1.6.11, with <i>A. trichopoda</i> as the root and the number of bootstraps set to 1000. We then used MCMCTee software in the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Yang, Z. PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Bioinformatics 13, 555â556 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3528">96</a></sup> v4.9i to calculate the divergence time. The number of iterations of the Markov chain included a burn-in number of 700,000, a sampfreq of 30, and an nSample of 6,000,000.</p><p>Using the results of the evolutionary tree with differentiation time and gene family clustering by CAFE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1987â1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3535">97</a></sup> v4.2, we estimated the number of gene family members of each branchâs ancestor via a birth mortality model, which was used to predict the contraction and expansion of gene families of the snake gourd relative to its ancestors. We determined whether the expansion or contraction was significant using a <i>P</i>-value cutoff of 0.05. The expanded and contracted gene families identified in snake gourd were annotated with PANTHER, and GO and KEGG enrichment analyses on these families were performed with ClusterProfile.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Collinearity and WGD analyses</h3><p>Diamond<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Buchfink, B., Xie, C. &amp; Huson, D. H. Fast and sensitive protein alignment using DIAMOND. Nat. Methods 12, 59â60 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3550">98</a></sup> v0.9.29.130 was used to compare the protein sequences of two species and determine similar gene pairs (<i>E</i>-valueâ&lt;â1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>, C scoreâ&gt;â0.5). Based on the diamond results, collinear blocks between the genomes of the two species were identified using MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3559">99</a></sup>.</p><p>WGD events were determined based on the distribution of the synonymous substitution rate (<i>Ks</i>) and fourfold degenerate (4DTv) sites of paralogous genes, which were calculated using WGD software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Zwaenepoel, A. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. wgd-simple command line tools for the analysis of ancient whole-genome duplications. Bioinformatics 35, 2153â2155 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3569">100</a></sup> in conjunction with a Perl script (<a href="https://github.com/JinfengChen/Scripts">https://github.com/JinfengChen/Scripts</a>), respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Transcriptome</h3><p>Fresh snake gourd fruit samples at 20, 40, and 60 d were collected for transcriptome analysis. cDNA libraries were obtained by PCR enrichment. After the library was checked for quality by quantitative PCR, the Illumina platform was used for sequencing. The data were cleaned by removing low-quality sequences and those containing adapter reads and used for sequence alignment with the specified reference genome. The transcriptome was assembled using StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290â295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3589">78</a></sup>. Differential expression analysis was performed between the different sample groups. Three replicates were used for each sample and DESeq2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550â550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00423-9#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d1853e3593">101</a></sup> was used for differential expression analysis between sample groups to obtain the DEG sets between two biological samples. During the detection of DEGs, a fold changeâ&gt;â2 and a false discovery rate (FDR)â&lt;â0.01 were used. As a screening standard, the fold change (0.01) represents the FDR between two samples by the use of a corrected <i>P</i>-value denoting significant differences. The gene functions were annotated on the basis of the content from six databases: the Nr, Pfam, KOG/COG, Swiss-Prot, KEGG, and GO databases.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Phylogenetic tree construction</h3><p>Predicted proteins from the genome were scanned using HMMER v3 (<a href="http://hmmer.org/download.html">http://hmmer.org/download.html</a>) employing the hidden Markov model (HMM) corresponding to the Pfam GH family. From the proteins obtained using the raw HMM, a high-quality protein set (<i>E</i>-valueâ&lt;â1âÃâ10âââ20 and manual verification of an intact domain) was aligned and used to construct a specific GH family HMM using hmmbuild from the HMMER v3 suite. With this new specific HMM, all proteins with an <i>E</i>-value lower than 0.01 were selected. The GH genes were further filtered based on manual curation and functional annotations (supplied by Beijing Biomarker).</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The raw genome and transcriptome sequencing data have been deposited into the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) database under BioProject accession numbers PRJNA640193 and PRJNA649380.</p>
